<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Extraterrestrial atmosphere</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For the view from an astronomical object's surface, see <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_skies" title="Extraterrestrial skies">Extraterrestrial skies</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Solar_sys.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="904" data-file-width="1440" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Solar_sys.jpg/300px-Solar_sys.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Solar_sys.jpg/450px-Solar_sys.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Solar_sys.jpg/600px-Solar_sys.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Solar_sys.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Major features of the Solar System (not to scale)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Solar_system_escape_velocity_vs_surface_temperature.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="512" height="275" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Solar_system_escape_velocity_vs_surface_temperature.svg/220px-Solar_system_escape_velocity_vs_surface_temperature.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Solar_system_escape_velocity_vs_surface_temperature.svg/330px-Solar_system_escape_velocity_vs_surface_temperature.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Solar_system_escape_velocity_vs_surface_temperature.svg/440px-Solar_system_escape_velocity_vs_surface_temperature.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Solar_system_escape_velocity_vs_surface_temperature.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Graphs of escape velocity against surface temperature of some Solar System objects showing which gases are retained. The objects are drawn to scale, and their data points are at the black dots in the middle.</div></div></div>
<p>The study of <b>extraterrestrial atmospheres</b> is an active field of research,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> both as an aspect of astronomy and to gain insight into Earth's atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> In addition to Earth, many of the other <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_object" title="Astronomical object">astronomical objects</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> have <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmospheres</a>. These include all the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gas_giants" title="Gas giants">gas giants</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mars" title="Atmosphere of Mars">Mars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Venus" title="Atmosphere of Venus">Venus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Pluto" title="Atmosphere of Pluto">Pluto</a>. Several <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">moons</a> and other bodies also have atmospheres, as do <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comets</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>. There is evidence that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extrasolar_planet" title="Extrasolar planet">extrasolar planets</a> can have an atmosphere. Comparisons of these atmospheres to one another and to Earth's atmosphere broaden our basic understanding of atmospheric processes such as the <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_effect" title="Greenhouse effect">greenhouse effect</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aerosol" title="Aerosol">aerosol</a> and cloud physics, and <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_chemistry" title="Atmospheric chemistry">atmospheric chemistry</a> and dynamics.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Planets">Planets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extraterrestrial_atmosphere&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Planets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inner_planets">Inner planets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extraterrestrial_atmosphere&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Inner planets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Earth is excluded from this enumeration because this article is about extraterrestrial atmospheres. For information about the terrestrial atmosphere, see <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">atmosphere of Earth</a>.</div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mercury">Mercury</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extraterrestrial_atmosphere&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Mercury">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mercury" title="Atmosphere of Mercury">Atmosphere of Mercury</a></div>
<p>Due to its small size (and thus its small gravity), <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a> has no substantial atmosphere. Its extremely thin atmosphere mostly consists of a small amount of helium and traces of sodium, potassium, and oxygen. These gases derive from the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a>, radioactive decay, meteor impacts, and breakdown of Mercury's crust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Mercury's atmosphere is not stable and is constantly being refreshed because of its atoms escaping into <a href="/wiki/Space" title="Space">space</a> as a result of the planet's heat.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Venus">Venus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extraterrestrial_atmosphere&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Venus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Venus" title="Atmosphere of Venus">Atmosphere of Venus</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Venuspioneeruv.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2158" data-file-width="2008" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Venuspioneeruv.jpg/220px-Venuspioneeruv.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Venuspioneeruv.jpg/330px-Venuspioneeruv.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Venuspioneeruv.jpg/440px-Venuspioneeruv.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Venuspioneeruv.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Atmosphere of Venus in UV, by <a href="/wiki/Pioneer_Venus_Orbiter" title="Pioneer Venus Orbiter">Pioneer Venus Orbiter</a> in 1979</div></div></div>
<p>Venus' atmosphere is mostly composed of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>. It contains minor amounts of <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> and other trace elements, including compounds based on <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>. The atmosphere of Venus is much hotter and denser than that of Earth, though shallower.  As greenhouse gases warm a lower atmosphere, they cool the upper atmosphere, leading to compact <a href="/wiki/Thermosphere" title="Thermosphere">thermospheres</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> By some definitions, Venus has no stratosphere.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Troposphere" title="Troposphere">troposphere</a> begins at the surface and extends up to an altitude of 65 kilometres (an altitude at which the <a href="/wiki/Mesosphere" title="Mesosphere">mesosphere</a> has already been reached on Earth). At the top of the troposphere, temperature and pressure reach Earth-like levels. Winds at the surface are a few metres per second, reaching 70 m/s or more in the upper troposphere. The <a href="/wiki/Stratosphere" title="Stratosphere">stratosphere</a> and mesosphere extend from 65 km to 95 km in height. The thermosphere and exosphere begin at around 95 kilometres, eventually reaching the limit of the atmosphere at about 220 to 250 km.
</p><p>The air pressure at Venus' surface is about 92 times that of the Earth. The enormous amount of CO<sub>2</sub> in the atmosphere creates a strong <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_effect" title="Greenhouse effect">greenhouse effect</a>, raising the surface temperature to around 470 °C, hotter than that of any other planet in the Solar System.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mars">Mars</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extraterrestrial_atmosphere&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Mars">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mars" title="Atmosphere of Mars">Atmosphere of Mars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Climate_of_Mars" title="Climate of Mars">Climate of Mars</a></div>
<p>The Martian atmosphere is very thin and composed mainly of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, with some <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a>. The average <a href="/wiki/Surface_pressure" title="Surface pressure">surface pressure</a> on Mars is 0.6-0.9 <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">kPa</a>, compared to about 101 kPa for Earth. This results in a much lower atmospheric <a href="/wiki/Volumetric_heat_capacity" title="Volumetric heat capacity">thermal inertia</a>, and as a consequence Mars is subject to strong <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_tide" title="Atmospheric tide">thermal tides</a> that can change total atmospheric pressure by up to 10%. The thin atmosphere also increases the variability of the planet's temperature. Martian surface temperatures vary from lows of approximately −140 °C (−220 °F) during the polar winters to highs of up to 20 °C (70 °F) in summers.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mars_atmosphere.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1349" data-file-width="1205" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Mars_atmosphere.jpg/200px-Mars_atmosphere.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Mars_atmosphere.jpg/300px-Mars_atmosphere.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Mars_atmosphere.jpg/400px-Mars_atmosphere.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mars_atmosphere.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The tenuous atmosphere of Mars visible on the horizon.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mars_pits_1999.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="251" data-file-width="503" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Mars_pits_1999.gif/200px-Mars_pits_1999.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Mars_pits_1999.gif/300px-Mars_pits_1999.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Mars_pits_1999.gif/400px-Mars_pits_1999.gif 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mars_pits_1999.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pits in south polar ice cap, MGS 1999, NASA</div></div></div>
<p>Between the <a href="/wiki/Viking_program" title="Viking program">Viking</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mars_Global_Surveyor" title="Mars Global Surveyor">Mars Global Surveyor</a> missions, Mars saw "Much colder (10-20 K) global atmospheric temperatures were observed during the 1997 versus 1977 perihelion periods" and "that the global aphelion atmosphere of Mars is colder, less dusty, and cloudier than indicated by the established Viking climatology,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> with "generally colder atmospheric temperatures and lower dust loading in recent decades on Mars than during the Viking Mission."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>  The <a href="/wiki/Mars_Reconnaissance_Orbiter" title="Mars Reconnaissance Orbiter">Mars Reconnaissance Orbiter</a>, though spanning a much shorter dataset, shows no warming of planetary average temperature, and a possible cooling.  "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mars_climate_sounder" title="Mars climate sounder">MCS</a> MY 28 temperatures are an average of 0.9 (daytime) and 1.7 K (night- time) cooler than <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermal_emission_spectrometer" title="Thermal emission spectrometer">TES</a> MY 24 measurements."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>  Locally and regionally, however, changes in <a href="/wiki/Swiss_cheese_features" title="Swiss cheese features">pits</a> in the layer of frozen <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> at the Martian south pole observed between 1999 and 2001 suggest the <a href="/wiki/Planum_Australe" title="Planum Australe">south polar ice cap</a> is shrinking. More recent observations indicate that Mars' south pole is continuing to melt. "It's evaporating right now at a prodigious rate," says <a href="/wiki/Michael_C._Malin" title="Michael C. Malin">Michael Malin</a>, principal investigator for the Mars Orbiter Camera.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> The pits in the ice are growing by about 3 meters (9.8 ft) per year. Malin states that conditions on <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> are not currently conductive to the formation of new ice. A web site has suggested that this indicates a "climate change in progress" on <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Multiple studies suggests this may be a local phenomenon rather than a global one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>Colin Wilson has proposed that the observed variations are caused by irregularities in the orbit of Mars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NG_Mars_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-NG_Mars-13">[13]</a></sup> William Feldman speculates the warming could be because Mars might be coming out of an <a href="/wiki/Ice_age" title="Ice age">ice age</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Other scientists state the warming may be a result of <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a> changes from dust storms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NG_Mars2_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-NG_Mars2-16">[16]</a></sup>  The study predicts the planet could continue to warm, as a result of <a href="/wiki/Positive_feedback" title="Positive feedback">positive feedback</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NG_Mars2_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-NG_Mars2-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>On June 7, 2018, NASA announced that the <a href="/wiki/Curiosity_(rover)" title="Curiosity (rover)"><i>Curiosity</i> rover</a> detected a cyclical seasonal variation in atmospheric <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, as well as the presence of <a href="/wiki/Kerogen" title="Kerogen">kerogen</a> and other complex <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20180607_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20180607-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20180607vid_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20180607vid-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SPC-20180607_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-SPC-20180607-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20180607_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20180607-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20180607_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20180607-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20180608a_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20180608a-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20180608b_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20180608b-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20180608c_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20180608c-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gas_giants">Gas giants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extraterrestrial_atmosphere&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Gas giants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The four outer planets of the Solar System are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gas_giants" title="Gas giants">gas giants</a>. They share some atmospheric commonalities. All have atmospheres that are mostly <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> and that blend into the liquid interior at pressures greater than the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Critical_pressure" title="Critical pressure">critical pressure</a>, so that there is no clear boundary between atmosphere and body.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Jupiter">Jupiter</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extraterrestrial_atmosphere&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Jupiter">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Jupiter" title="Atmosphere of Jupiter">Atmosphere of Jupiter</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Redjunior.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="808" data-file-width="1500" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Redjunior.jpg/275px-Redjunior.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Redjunior.jpg/413px-Redjunior.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Redjunior.jpg/550px-Redjunior.jpg 2x" width="275"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Redjunior.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Oval BA on the left and the Great Red Spot on the right</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>'s upper atmosphere is composed of about 75% hydrogen and 24% helium by mass, with the remaining 1% consisting of other elements. The interior contains denser materials such that the distribution is roughly 71% hydrogen, 24% helium and 5% other elements by mass. The atmosphere contains trace amounts of <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">water vapor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a>-based compounds. There are also traces of <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethane" title="Ethane">ethane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neon" title="Neon">neon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phosphine" title="Phosphine">phosphine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>. The outermost layer of the atmosphere contains <a href="/wiki/Crystal" title="Crystal">crystals</a> of frozen ammonia, possibly underlaid by a thin layer of <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>.
</p><p>Jupiter is covered with a cloud layer about 50 km deep. The clouds are composed of <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> crystals and possibly ammonium hydrosulfide. The clouds are located in the <a href="/wiki/Tropopause" title="Tropopause">tropopause</a> and are arranged into bands of different <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitudes</a>, known as tropical regions. These are sub-divided into lighter-hued <i>zones</i> and darker <i>belts</i>. The interactions of these conflicting <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_circulation" title="Atmospheric circulation">circulation</a> patterns cause storms and <a href="/wiki/Turbulence" title="Turbulence">turbulence</a>. The best-known feature of the cloud layer is the <a href="/wiki/Great_Red_Spot" title="Great Red Spot">Great Red Spot</a>, a persistent <a href="/wiki/Anticyclone" title="Anticyclone">anticyclonic</a> <a href="/wiki/Storm" title="Storm">storm</a> located 22° south of the equator that is larger than Earth. In 2000, an atmospheric feature formed in the southern hemisphere that is similar in appearance to the Great Red Spot, but smaller in size. The feature was named <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oval_BA" title="Oval BA">Oval BA</a>, and has been nicknamed Red Spot Junior.
</p><p>Observations of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oval_BA" title="Oval BA">Red Spot Jr.</a> storm suggest <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> could be in a period of global climate change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> This is hypothesized to be part of an approximately 70 year global climate cycle, characterized by the relatively rapid forming and subsequent slow erosion and merging of cyclonic and anticyclonic <a href="/wiki/Vortex" title="Vortex">vortices</a> in Jupiter's atmosphere. These vortices facilitate the heat exchange between poles and equator. If they have sufficiently eroded, heat exchange is strongly reduced and regional temperatures may shift by as much as 10 K, with the poles cooling down and the equator region heating up. The resulting large temperature differential destabilizes the atmosphere and thereby leads to the creation of new vortices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Saturn">Saturn</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extraterrestrial_atmosphere&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Saturn">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The outer atmosphere of <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a> consists of about 93.2% hydrogen and 6.7% helium. Trace amounts of ammonia, <a href="/wiki/Acetylene" title="Acetylene">acetylene</a>, ethane, phosphine, and methane have also been detected. As with Jupiter, the upper clouds on Saturn are composed of ammonia crystals, while the lower level clouds appear to be composed of either <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_hydrosulfide" title="Ammonium hydrosulfide">ammonium hydrosulfide</a> (NH<sub>4</sub>SH) or water.
</p><p>The Saturnian atmosphere is in several ways similar to that of Jupiter. It exhibits a banded pattern similar to Jupiter's, and occasionally exhibits long-lived ovals caused by storms. A storm formation analogous to Jupiter's Great Red Spot, the Great White Spot, is a short-lived phenomenon that forms with a roughly 30-year periodicity. It was last observed in 1990. However, the storms and the band pattern are less visible and active than those of Jupiter, due to the overlying ammonia hazes in Saturn's troposphere.
</p><p>Saturn's atmosphere has several unusual features. Its winds are among the Solar System's fastest, with <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">Voyager</a> data indicating peak easterly winds of 500 m/s. It is also the only planet with a warm polar vortex, and is the only planet other than Earth where <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eyewall" title="Eyewall">eyewall</a> clouds have been observed in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hurricane" title="Hurricane">hurricane</a>-like structures.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Uranus">Uranus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extraterrestrial_atmosphere&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Uranus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Uranus" title="Atmosphere of Uranus">Atmosphere of Uranus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Climate_of_Uranus" title="Climate of Uranus">Climate of Uranus</a></div>
<p>The atmosphere of <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a> is composed primarily of gas and various ices. It is about 83% hydrogen, 15% helium, 2% methane and traces of acetylene. Like Jupiter and Saturn, Uranus has a banded cloud layer, although this is not readily visible without enhancement of visual images of the planet. Unlike the larger gas giants, the low temperatures in the upper Uranian cloud layer, down to 50 <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">K</a>, causes cloud formation from methane rather than ammonia.
</p><p>Less storm activity has been observed in the Uranian atmosphere than in those of Jupiter or Saturn, due to the overlying methane and acetylene hazes in its atmosphere making the planet look like a bland, light blue globe.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Images taken in 1997 with the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a> showed storm activity in that part of the atmosphere emerging from the 25-year-long Uranian winter. The general lack of storm activity may be related to the lack of an internal energy generation mechanism for Uranus, a feature unique among the gas giants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Neptune">Neptune</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extraterrestrial_atmosphere&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Neptune">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Neptune_storms.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="870" data-file-width="945" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Neptune_storms.jpg/150px-Neptune_storms.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Neptune_storms.jpg/225px-Neptune_storms.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Neptune_storms.jpg/300px-Neptune_storms.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Neptune_storms.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Great_Dark_Spot" title="Great Dark Spot">Great Dark Spot</a> (top), <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Scooter_(spot_on_Neptune)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Scooter (spot on Neptune) (page does not exist)">Scooter</a> (middle white cloud), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wizard%27s_eye" title="Wizard's eye">Wizard's eye</a>/Dark Spot 2 (bottom).</div></div></div>
<p>The atmosphere of <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a> is similar to that of Uranus. It is about 80% hydrogen, 19% helium, and 1.5% methane. However the weather activity on Neptune is much more active, and its atmosphere is much bluer than that of Uranus. The upper levels of the atmosphere reach temperatures of about 55 <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">K</a>, giving rise to methane clouds in its troposphere, which gives the planet its ultramarine color. Temperatures rise steadily deeper inside the atmosphere.
</p><p>Neptune has extremely dynamic weather systems, including the highest wind speeds in the Solar System, thought to be powered by the flow of internal heat. Typical winds in the banded equatorial region can possess speeds of around 350 m/s, while storm systems can have winds reaching up to around 900 m/s, nearly the speed of sound in Neptune's atmosphere. Several large storm systems have been identified, including the Great Dark Spot, a cyclonic storm system the size of Eurasia, the Scooter, a white cloud group further south than the Great Dark Spot, and the Wizard's eye/Dark Spot 2, a southern cyclonic storm.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>, the farthest <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planet</a> from Earth, has increased in brightness since 1980. Neptune's brightness is statistically correlated with its stratospheric temperature. Hammel and Lockwood hypothesize that the change in brightness includes a solar variation component as well as a seasonal component, though they did not find a statistically significant correlation with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_variation" title="Solar variation">solar variation</a>. They propose that the resolution of this issue will be clarified by brightness observations in the next few years: forcing by a change in sub-solar latitude should be reflected in a flattening and decline of brightness, while solar forcing should be reflected in a flattening and then resumed rise of brightness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_bodies_in_the_Solar_System">Other bodies in the Solar System</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extraterrestrial_atmosphere&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Other bodies in the Solar System">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_satellites">Natural satellites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extraterrestrial_atmosphere&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Natural satellites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Ten of the many natural satellites in the Solar System are known to have atmospheres: <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">Io</a>, <a href="/wiki/Callisto_(moon)" title="Callisto (moon)">Callisto</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enceladus_(moon)" title="Enceladus (moon)">Enceladus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a>, <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhea_(moon)" title="Rhea (moon)">Rhea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dione_(moon)" title="Dione (moon)">Dione</a>, <a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">Triton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>. Ganymede and Europa both have very tenuous oxygen atmospheres, thought to be produced by radiation splitting the water ice present on the surface of these moons into hydrogen and oxygen. Io has an extremely thin atmosphere consisting mainly of sulfur dioxide (<span class="chemf nowrap">SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>), arising from volcanism and sunlight-driven sublimation of surface sulfur dioxide deposits. The atmosphere of Enceladus is also extremely thin and variable, consisting mainly of water vapor, nitrogen, methane, and carbon dioxide vented from the moon's interior through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cryovolcanism" title="Cryovolcanism">cryovolcanism</a>. The extremely thin carbon dioxide atmosphere of Callisto is thought to be replenished by sublimation from surface deposits.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Moon">Moon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extraterrestrial_atmosphere&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Moon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_the_Moon" title="Atmosphere of the Moon">Atmosphere of the Moon</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Titan">Titan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extraterrestrial_atmosphere&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Titan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Titan" title="Atmosphere of Titan">Atmosphere of Titan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Titan-Complex_%27Anti-greenhouse%27.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1004" data-file-width="972" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Titan-Complex_%27Anti-greenhouse%27.jpg/220px-Titan-Complex_%27Anti-greenhouse%27.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Titan-Complex_%27Anti-greenhouse%27.jpg/330px-Titan-Complex_%27Anti-greenhouse%27.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Titan-Complex_%27Anti-greenhouse%27.jpg/440px-Titan-Complex_%27Anti-greenhouse%27.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Titan-Complex_%27Anti-greenhouse%27.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>True-color image of layers of haze in Titan's atmosphere.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a> has by far the densest atmosphere of any moon. The Titanese atmosphere is in fact denser than <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>'s, with a surface pressure of 147 <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">kPa</a>, one and a half times that of the Earth. The atmosphere is 98.4% <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, with the remaining 1.6% composed of <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> and trace amounts of other gases such as hydrocarbons (including <a href="/wiki/Ethane" title="Ethane">ethane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diacetylene" title="Diacetylene">diacetylene</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methylacetylene" title="Methylacetylene">methylacetylene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cyanoacetylene" title="Cyanoacetylene">cyanoacetylene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acetylene" title="Acetylene">acetylene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Propane" title="Propane">propane</a>), <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cyanogen" title="Cyanogen">cyanogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_cyanide" title="Hydrogen cyanide">hydrogen cyanide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>. The hydrocarbons are thought to form in Titan's upper atmosphere in reactions resulting from the breakup of methane by the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> light, producing a thick orange smog. Titan has no <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a> and sometimes orbits outside Saturn's <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetosphere</a>, directly exposing it to the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a>. This may <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ionize</a> and carry away some molecules from the top of the atmosphere.
</p><p>Titan's atmosphere supports an opaque cloud layer that obscures Titan's surface features at visible wavelengths. The <a href="/wiki/Haze" title="Haze">haze</a> that can be seen in the adjacent picture contributes to the moon's <a href="/wiki/Anti-greenhouse_effect" title="Anti-greenhouse effect">anti-greenhouse effect</a> and lowers the temperature by reflecting sunlight away from the satellite. The thick atmosphere blocks most visible wavelength light from the Sun and other sources from reaching Titan's surface.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Triton">Triton</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extraterrestrial_atmosphere&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Triton">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Triton" title="Atmosphere of Triton">Atmosphere of Triton</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">Triton</a>, Neptune's largest moon, has a tenuous nitrogen atmosphere with small amounts of methane. Tritonian atmospheric pressure is about 1<a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">Pa</a>. The surface temperature is at least 35.6 K, with the nitrogen atmosphere in equilibrium with <a href="/wiki/Solid_nitrogen" title="Solid nitrogen">nitrogen ice</a> on Triton's surface.
</p><p>Triton has increased in absolute temperature by 5% since 1989 to 1998.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MIT_Triton_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-MIT_Triton-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> A similar rise of temperature on Earth would be equal to about 11 °C (20 °F) increase in temperature in nine years. "At least since 1989, Triton has been undergoing a period of global warming. Percentage-wise, it's a very large increase," said <a href="/wiki/James_L._Elliot" title="James L. Elliot">James L. Elliot</a>, who published the report.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MIT_Triton_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-MIT_Triton-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>Triton is approaching an unusually warm summer season that only happens once every few hundred years. Elliot and his colleagues believe that Triton's warming trend could be driven by seasonal changes in the absorption of solar energy by its polar ice caps. One suggestion for this warming is that it is a result of frost patterns changing on its surface. Another is that ice albedo has changed, allowing for more heat from the Sun to be absorbed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Bonnie J. Buratti <i>et al.</i> argue the changes in temperature are a result of deposition of dark, red material from geological processes on the moon, such as massive venting. Because Triton's <a href="/wiki/Bond_albedo" title="Bond albedo">Bond albedo</a> is among the highest within the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>, it is sensitive to small variations in spectral <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pluto">Pluto</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extraterrestrial_atmosphere&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Pluto">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Pluto" title="Atmosphere of Pluto">Atmosphere of Pluto</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:254px;max-width:254px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:252px;max-width:252px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA19948-NH-Pluto-Norgay-Hillary-Mountains-2050714.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="3652" data-file-width="6797" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/PIA19948-NH-Pluto-Norgay-Hillary-Mountains-2050714.jpg/250px-PIA19948-NH-Pluto-Norgay-Hillary-Mountains-2050714.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/PIA19948-NH-Pluto-Norgay-Hillary-Mountains-2050714.jpg/375px-PIA19948-NH-Pluto-Norgay-Hillary-Mountains-2050714.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/PIA19948-NH-Pluto-Norgay-Hillary-Mountains-2050714.jpg/500px-PIA19948-NH-Pluto-Norgay-Hillary-Mountains-2050714.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:252px;max-width:252px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA19947-NH-Pluto-Norgay-Hillary-Mountains-20150714.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1321" data-file-width="2055" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/PIA19947-NH-Pluto-Norgay-Hillary-Mountains-20150714.jpg/250px-PIA19947-NH-Pluto-Norgay-Hillary-Mountains-20150714.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/PIA19947-NH-Pluto-Norgay-Hillary-Mountains-20150714.jpg/375px-PIA19947-NH-Pluto-Norgay-Hillary-Mountains-20150714.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/PIA19947-NH-Pluto-Norgay-Hillary-Mountains-20150714.jpg/500px-PIA19947-NH-Pluto-Norgay-Hillary-Mountains-20150714.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a></div></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><center><a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a> - <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norgay_Montes" title="Norgay Montes">Norgay Montes</a> (left-foreground); <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Montes" title="Hillary Montes">Hillary Montes</a> (left-skyline); <a href="/wiki/Sputnik_Planitia" title="Sputnik Planitia">Sputnik Planitia</a> (right)<br/>Near-sunset view includes several layers of <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Pluto" title="Atmosphere of Pluto">atmospheric haze</a>.</center></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a> has an extremely thin  atmosphere that consists of <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>, derived from the ices on its surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>  Two models <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> show that the atmosphere does not completely freeze and collapse when Pluto moves further from the Sun on its extremely <a href="/wiki/Elliptic_orbit" title="Elliptic orbit">elliptical orbit</a>. However, some other models do show this. Pluto needs 248 years for one complete orbit, and has been observed for less than one third of that time. It has an average distance of 39 <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a> from the Sun, hence in-depth data from Pluto is sparse and difficult to gather. Temperature is inferred indirectly for Pluto; when it passes in front of a star, observers note how fast the light drops off. From this, they deduce the density of the atmosphere, and that is used as an indicator of temperature.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NH-Pluto-Atmosphere-20150810.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="652" data-file-width="777" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/NH-Pluto-Atmosphere-20150810.jpg/150px-NH-Pluto-Atmosphere-20150810.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/NH-Pluto-Atmosphere-20150810.jpg/225px-NH-Pluto-Atmosphere-20150810.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/NH-Pluto-Atmosphere-20150810.jpg/300px-NH-Pluto-Atmosphere-20150810.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NH-Pluto-Atmosphere-20150810.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pluto's atmosphere backlit by the Sun</div></div></div>
<p>One such <a href="/wiki/Occultation" title="Occultation">occultation</a> event happened in 1988. Observations of a second occulation on August 20, 2002 suggest that Pluto's atmospheric pressure has tripled, indicating a warming of about 2  °C (3.6  °F),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pluto_warm_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pluto_warm-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> as predicted by Hansen and Paige.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> The warming is "likely not connected with that of the Earth," says Jay Pasachoff.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
One astronomer has speculated the warming may be a result of eruptive activity, but it is more likely Pluto's temperature is heavily influenced by its elliptical orbit. It was closest to the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> in 1989 (<a href="/wiki/Apsis" title="Apsis">perihelion</a>) and has slowly receded since. If it has any thermal inertia, it is expected to warm for a while after it passes perihelion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> "This warming trend on Pluto could easily last for another 13 years," says <a href="/wiki/David_J._Tholen" title="David J. Tholen">David J. Tholen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pluto_warm_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pluto_warm-38">[38]</a></sup> It has also been suggested that a darkening of surface ice may also be the cause, but additional data and modeling is needed. Frost distribution on the surface of Pluto is significantly affected by the dwarf planet's high obliquity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Exoplanets">Exoplanets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extraterrestrial_atmosphere&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Exoplanets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:17pHolmes_071104_eder_vga.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="416" data-file-width="640" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/17pHolmes_071104_eder_vga.jpg/200px-17pHolmes_071104_eder_vga.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/17pHolmes_071104_eder_vga.jpg/300px-17pHolmes_071104_eder_vga.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/17pHolmes_071104_eder_vga.jpg/400px-17pHolmes_071104_eder_vga.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:17pHolmes_071104_eder_vga.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Telescopic image of Comet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/17P/Holmes" title="17P/Holmes">17P/Holmes</a> in 2007</div></div></div>
<p>Several planets outside the Solar System (<a href="/wiki/Exoplanet" title="Exoplanet">exoplanets</a>) have been observed to have atmospheres. At the present time, most atmosphere detections are of hot Jupiters or hot Neptunes that orbit very close to their star and thus have heated and extended atmospheres. Observations of exoplanet atmospheres are of two types. First, transmission photometry or spectra detect the light that passes through a planet's atmosphere as it transits in front of its star.  Second, the direct emission from a planet atmosphere may be detected by differencing the star plus planet light obtained during most of the planet's orbit with the light of just the star during secondary eclipse (when the exoplanet is behind its star).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The first observed extrasolar planetary atmosphere was made in 2001.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Sodium in the atmosphere of the planet <a href="/wiki/HD_209458_b" title="HD 209458 b">HD 209458 b</a> was detected during a set of four transits of the planet across its star. Later observations with the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a> showed an enormous <a href="/wiki/Ellipsoid" title="Ellipsoid">ellipsoidal</a> envelope of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> around the planet. This envelope reaches temperatures of 10,000 K. The planet is estimated to be losing (1-5)×10<sup>8</sup> kg of hydrogen per second. This type of atmosphere loss may be common to all planets orbiting Sun-like stars closer than around 0.1 AU.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-loss_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-loss-45">[45]</a></sup> In addition to hydrogen, carbon, and oxygen, HD 209458 b is thought to have <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">water vapor</a> in its atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Space.com_water_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Space.com_water-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Sodium and water vapour has also been observed in the atmosphere of <a href="/wiki/HD_189733_b" title="HD 189733 b">HD 189733 b</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Skh_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Skh-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> another hot gas giant planet.
</p><p>In October 2013, the detection of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clouds" title="Clouds">clouds</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a> of <a href="/wiki/Kepler-7b" title="Kepler-7b">Kepler-7b</a> was announced,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MIT-20131002_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-MIT-20131002-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ArXiv-20130930_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-ArXiv-20130930-52">[52]</a></sup> and, in December 2013, also in the atmospheres of <a href="/wiki/Gliese_436_b" title="Gliese 436 b">Gliese 436 b</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gliese_1214_b" title="Gliese 1214 b">Gliese 1214 b</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20131231_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20131231-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20140101a_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20140101a-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20140101b_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20140101b-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20140101c_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20140101c-56">[56]</a></sup>
In April 2017, an atmosphere was confirmed around <a href="/wiki/Gliese_1132_b" title="Gliese 1132 b">Gliese 1132 b</a>. It is the most Earth-like exoplanet to date that is known to have an atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>In May 2017, glints of light from <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, seen as twinkling from an orbiting satellite a million miles away, were found to be <a href="/wiki/Reflection_(physics)" title="Reflection (physics)">reflected light</a> from <a href="/wiki/Ice_crystals" title="Ice crystals">ice crystals</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">atmosphere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20170519_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20170519-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRL-201760515_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-GRL-201760515-59">[59]</a></sup> The technology used to determine this may be useful in studying the atmospheres of distant worlds, including those of exoplanets.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atmospheric_composition">Atmospheric composition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extraterrestrial_atmosphere&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Atmospheric composition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2001, <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a> was detected in the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a> of <a href="/wiki/HD_209458_b" title="HD 209458 b">HD 209458 b</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2008, <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> were detected in the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a> of <a href="/wiki/HD_189733_b" title="HD 189733 b">HD 189733 b</a>.
</p><p>In 2013, <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> was detected in the atmospheres of HD 209458 b, <a href="/wiki/XO-1b" title="XO-1b">XO-1b</a>, <a href="/wiki/WASP-12b" title="WASP-12b">WASP-12b</a>, <a href="/wiki/WASP-17b" title="WASP-17b">WASP-17b</a>, and <a href="/wiki/WASP-19b" title="WASP-19b">WASP-19b</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20131203_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20131203-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ApJ-20130910_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-ApJ-20130910-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ApJ-20131203_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-ApJ-20131203-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>In July 2014, NASA announced finding very dry <a href="/wiki/Exoplanet#Atmosphere" title="Exoplanet">atmospheres</a> on three exoplanets (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HD_189733b" title="HD 189733b">HD 189733b</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HD_209458b" title="HD 209458b">HD 209458b</a>, <a href="/wiki/WASP-12b" title="WASP-12b">WASP-12b</a>) orbiting Sun-like stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140724_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140724-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>In September 2014, NASA reported that <a href="/wiki/HAT-P-11b" title="HAT-P-11b">HAT-P-11b</a> is the first Neptune-sized exoplanet known to have a relatively cloud-free atmosphere and, as well, the first time <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecules</a> of any kind have been found, specifically <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">water vapor</a>, on such a relatively small exoplanet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140924_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140924-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>The presence of molecular <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>) may be detectable by ground-based telescopes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> and it can be produced by geophysical processes, as well as a byproduct of <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a> by life forms, so although encouraging, <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> is not a reliable <a href="/wiki/Biosignature" title="Biosignature">biosignature</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-physorg_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-physorg-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Luger_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Luger-71">[71]</a></sup> In fact, planets with high concentration of <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> in their atmosphere may be uninhabitable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Luger_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-Luger-71">[71]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis" title="Abiogenesis">Abiogenesis</a> in the presence of massive amounts of atmospheric oxygen could be difficult because early organisms relied on the free energy available in <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">redox reactions</a> involving a variety of hydrogen compounds; on an <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>-rich planet, organisms would have to compete with the oxygen for this free energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Luger_71-2"><a href="#cite_note-Luger-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>In June 2015, NASA reported that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WASP-33b" title="WASP-33b">WASP-33b</a> has a <a href="/wiki/Stratosphere" title="Stratosphere">stratosphere</a>. <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">Ozone</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrocarbons" title="Hydrocarbons">hydrocarbons</a> absorb large amounts of ultraviolet radiation, heating the upper parts of atmosphere's that contain them, creating a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperature_inversion" title="Temperature inversion">temperature inversion</a> and a stratosphere. However, these molecules are destroyed at the temperatures of hot exoplanets, creating doubt if the hot exoplanets could have a stratosphere. A temperature inversion, and stratosphere was identified on WASP-33b caused by <a href="/wiki/Titanium_oxide" title="Titanium oxide">titanium oxide</a>, which is a strong absorber of visible and ultraviolet radiation, and can only exist as a gas in a hot atmosphere.  WASP-33b is the hottest exoplanet known, with a temperature of 3,200 °C (5,790 °F)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> and is approximately four and a half times the mass of Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>In February 2016, it was announced that <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a> had detected <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> (and suggestions of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_cyanide" title="Hydrogen cyanide">hydrogen cyanide</a>), but no <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">water vapor</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a> of <a href="/wiki/55_Cancri_e" title="55 Cancri e">55 Cancri e</a>, the first time the atmosphere of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Super-earth" title="Super-earth">super-earth</a> exoplanet was analyzed successfully.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phys-20160216_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phys-20160216-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atmospheric_circulation">Atmospheric circulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extraterrestrial_atmosphere&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Atmospheric circulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The atmospheric circulation of planets that rotate more slowly or have a thicker atmosphere allows more heat to flow to the poles which reduces the temperature differences between the poles and the equator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Clouds">Clouds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extraterrestrial_atmosphere&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Clouds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In October 2013, the detection of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clouds" title="Clouds">clouds</a> in the atmosphere of <a href="/wiki/Kepler-7b" title="Kepler-7b">Kepler-7b</a> was announced,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MIT-20131002_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-MIT-20131002-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ArXiv-20130930_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-ArXiv-20130930-52">[52]</a></sup> and, in December 2013, also in the atmospheres of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GJ_436_b" title="GJ 436 b">GJ 436 b</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GJ_1214_b" title="GJ 1214 b">GJ 1214 b</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20131231_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20131231-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20140101a_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20140101a-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20140101b_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20140101b-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20140101c_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20140101c-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Precipitation">Precipitation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extraterrestrial_atmosphere&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Precipitation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Precipitation" title="Precipitation">Precipitation</a> in the form of liquid (rain) or solid (snow) varies in composition depending on atmospheric temperature, pressure, composition, and <a href="/wiki/Altitude" title="Altitude">altitude</a>. Hot atmospheres could have iron rain,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> molten-glass rain,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> and rain made from rocky minerals such as enstatite, corundum, spinel, and wollastonite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Deep in the atmospheres of gas giants, it could rain diamonds<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> and helium containing dissolved neon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Abiotic_oxygen">Abiotic oxygen</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extraterrestrial_atmosphere&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Abiotic oxygen">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are geological and atmospheric processes that produce free oxygen, so the detection of oxygen is not necessarily an indication of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAOJ2014_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAOJ2014-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>The processes of life result in a mixture of chemicals that are not in <a href="/wiki/Chemical_equilibrium" title="Chemical equilibrium">chemical equilibrium</a> but there are also abiotic disequilibrium processes that need to be considered. The most robust atmospheric <a href="/wiki/Biosignature" title="Biosignature">biosignature</a> is often considered to be molecular <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>) and its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photochemical" title="Photochemical">photochemical</a> byproduct <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">ozone</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>). The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photolysis" title="Photolysis">photolysis</a> of water (<span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>) by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UV_rays" title="UV rays">UV rays</a> followed by <a href="/wiki/Hydrodynamic_escape" title="Hydrodynamic escape">hydrodynamic escape</a> of hydrogen can lead to a build-up of oxygen in planets close to their star undergoing <a href="/wiki/Runaway_greenhouse_effect" title="Runaway greenhouse effect">runaway greenhouse effect</a>. For planets in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habitable_zone" title="Habitable zone">habitable zone</a>, it was thought that water photolysis would be strongly limited by <a href="/wiki/Cold_trap_(astronomy)" title="Cold trap (astronomy)">cold-trapping</a> of water vapour in the lower atmosphere. However, the extent of H<sub>2</sub>O cold-trapping depends strongly on the amount of non-<a href="/wiki/Condensation" title="Condensation">condensible</a> gases in the atmosphere such as <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> N<sub>2</sub> and <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a>. In the absence of such gases, the likelihood of build-up of oxygen also depends in complex ways on the planet's accretion history, internal chemistry, atmospheric dynamics, and orbital state. Therefore, oxygen, on its own, cannot be considered a robust biosignature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> The ratio of nitrogen and argon to oxygen could be detected by studying <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">thermal</a> <a href="/wiki/Phase_curve_(astronomy)" title="Phase curve (astronomy)">phase curves</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> or by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transit_method" title="Transit method">transit</a> transmission spectroscopy measurement of the spectral <a href="/wiki/Rayleigh_scattering" title="Rayleigh scattering">Rayleigh scattering</a> slope in a clear-sky (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Aerosol" title="Aerosol">aerosol</a>-free) atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Methane">Methane</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extraterrestrial_atmosphere&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Methane">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Detection of methane in astronomical bodies is of interest to science and technology, as it may be evidence of extraterrestrial life (<a href="/wiki/Biosignature" title="Biosignature">biosignature</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atreya_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Atreya-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> it may help provide organic ingredients <a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis" title="Abiogenesis">for life to form</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atreya_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-Atreya-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> and also, methane could be used as a fuel or rocket propellant for future robotic and crewed missions in the Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a> – the tenuous atmosphere contains trace amounts of methane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a> – the atmosphere contains a large amount of methane from 60 km (37 mi) to the surface according to data collected by the <a href="/wiki/Pioneer_Venus_project" title="Pioneer Venus project">Pioneer Venus</a> Large Probe Neutral <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_Spectrometer" title="Mass Spectrometer">Mass Spectrometer</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> – traces are outgassed from the surface<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA19088-MarsCuriosityRover-MethaneSource-20141216.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="1440" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/PIA19088-MarsCuriosityRover-MethaneSource-20141216.png/220px-PIA19088-MarsCuriosityRover-MethaneSource-20141216.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/PIA19088-MarsCuriosityRover-MethaneSource-20141216.png/330px-PIA19088-MarsCuriosityRover-MethaneSource-20141216.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/PIA19088-MarsCuriosityRover-MethaneSource-20141216.png/440px-PIA19088-MarsCuriosityRover-MethaneSource-20141216.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA19088-MarsCuriosityRover-MethaneSource-20141216.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mars#Methane" title="Atmosphere of Mars">Methane</a> (CH<sub>4</sub>) on Mars – potential sources and sinks.</div></div></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> – the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mars#Methane" title="Atmosphere of Mars">Martian atmosphere</a> contains 10 nmol/<a href="/wiki/Mole_(unit)" title="Mole (unit)">mol</a> methane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-methane_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-methane-95">[95]</a></sup> The source of methane on Mars has not been determined. Research suggests that methane may come from <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanoes</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fault_line" title="Fault line">fault lines</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Methanogen" title="Methanogen">methanogens</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> that it may be a byproduct of electrical discharges from <a href="/wiki/Dust_devil" title="Dust devil">dust devils</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dust_storm" title="Dust storm">dust storms</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> or that it may be the result of <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">UV</a> <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">radiation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> In January 2009, NASA scientists announced that they had discovered that the planet often vents methane into the atmosphere in specific areas, leading some to speculate this may be a sign of biological activity below the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Curiosity_rover" title="Curiosity rover"><i>Curiosity</i> rover</a>, which landed on Mars in August 2012, can distinguish between different <a href="/wiki/Isotopologue" title="Isotopologue">isotopologues</a> of methane;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> but even if the mission determines that microscopic Martian life is the source of the methane, it probably resides far below the surface, beyond the rover's reach.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> The first measurements with the <a href="/wiki/Sample_Analysis_at_Mars" title="Sample Analysis at Mars">Tunable Laser Spectrometer (TLS)</a> indicated that there is less than 5 ppb of methane at the landing site.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Science-20121102_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Science-20121102-103">[103]</a></sup> On 16 December 2014, NASA reported the Curiosity rover detected a "tenfold spike", likely localized, in the amount of methane in the Martian atmosphere. Sample measurements taken "a dozen times over 20 months" showed increases in late 2013 and early 2014, averaging "7 parts of methane per billion in the atmosphere." Before and after that, readings averaged around one-tenth that level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20141216-GW_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20141216-GW-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20141216-KC_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20141216-KC-105">[105]</a></sup> The spikes in concentration suggest that Mars is episodically producing or releasing methane from an unknown source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Webster_2015_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Webster_2015-106">[106]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/ExoMars_Trace_Gas_Orbiter" title="ExoMars Trace Gas Orbiter">ExoMars Trace Gas Orbiter</a> will perform measurements of methane starting in April 2018, as well as its decomposition products such as <a href="/wiki/Formaldehyde" title="Formaldehyde">formaldehyde</a> and <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> – the atmosphere contains 3000 ± 1000 ppm methane<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a> – the atmosphere contains 4500 ± 2000 ppm methane<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enceladus_(moon)" title="Enceladus (moon)">Enceladus</a> – the atmosphere contains 1.7% methane<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Waite_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Waite-109">[109]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Iapetus_(moon)" title="Iapetus (moon)">Iapetus</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a> –  the atmosphere contains 1.6% methane and thousands of methane lakes have been detected on the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Niemann_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Niemann-110">[110]</a></sup> In the upper atmosphere, methane is converted into more complex molecules including <a href="/wiki/Acetylene" title="Acetylene">acetylene</a>, a process that also produces molecular <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>. There is evidence that acetylene and hydrogen are recycled into methane near the surface. This suggests the presence either of an exotic catalyst, possibly an unknown form of methanogenic life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-life_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-life-111">[111]</a></sup> Methane showers, probably prompted by changing seasons, have also been observed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> On October 24, 2014, methane was found in polar clouds on Titan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20141024-PD_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20141024-PD-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20141024-EZ_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20141024-EZ-114">[114]</a></sup></li></ul></li></ul>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; overflow:hidden; width:auto; max-width:608px;">
